</td></tr></tbody></table></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_equipment">Sports equipment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zoom_elite_2.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zoom_elite_2.png/220px-Zoom_elite_2.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2206" data-file-height="1018"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 102px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Zoom_elite_2.png/220px-Zoom_elite_2.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="102" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zoom_elite_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nike Zoom Elite 2 <a href="/wiki/Sneakers" title="Sneakers">athletic shoe</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Airjordan1985-1st.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Airjordan1985-1st.JPG/220px-Airjordan1985-1st.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="296" data-file-height="252"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 187px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Airjordan1985-1st.JPG/220px-Airjordan1985-1st.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="187" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Airjordan1985-1st.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of Nike Air Jordan I basketball shoes</div></div></div>
<p>Nike produces a wide range of sports equipment. Their first products were track running shoes. They currently also make shoes, jerseys, shorts, <a href="/wiki/Cleat_(shoe)" title="Cleat (shoe)">cleats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baselayers" class="mw-redirect" title="Baselayers">baselayers</a>, etc. for a wide range of sports, including track and field, baseball, <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a>, tennis, association football (soccer), <a href="/wiki/Lacrosse" title="Lacrosse">lacrosse</a>, basketball, and <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nike_Air_Max" title="Nike Air Max">Nike Air Max</a> is a line of shoes first released by Nike, Inc. in 1987. Additional product lines were introduced later, such as Air Huarache, which debuted in 1992. The most recent additions to their line are the Nike 6.0, Nike NYX, and <a href="/wiki/Nike_SB" class="mw-redirect" title="Nike SB">Nike SB</a> shoes, designed for <a href="/wiki/Skateboarding" title="Skateboarding">skateboarding</a>.  Nike has recently introduced cricket shoes called Air Zoom Yorker, designed to be 30% lighter than their competitors'.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>  In 2008, Nike introduced the Air Jordan XX3, a high-performance basketball shoe designed with the environment in mind.
</p><p>Nike sells an assortment of products, including shoes and apparel for sports activities like association football,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> basketball, running, <a href="/wiki/Combat_sports" class="mw-redirect" title="Combat sports">combat sports</a>, tennis, <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a>, athletics, golf, and <a href="/wiki/Cross_training" class="mw-redirect" title="Cross training">cross training</a> for men, women, and children. Nike also sells shoes for outdoor activities such as tennis, golf, <a href="/wiki/Skateboarding" title="Skateboarding">skateboarding</a>, association football, baseball, <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a>, cycling, volleyball, <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheerleading" title="Cheerleading">cheerleading</a>, aquatic activities, auto racing, and other athletic and recreational uses.  Nike recently teamed up with <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a> to produce the <a href="/wiki/Nike%2BiPod" class="mw-redirect" title="Nike+iPod">Nike+</a> product that monitors a runner's performance via a radio device in the shoe that links to the <a href="/wiki/IPod_nano" class="mw-redirect" title="IPod nano">iPod nano</a>.  While the product generates useful statistics, it has been criticized by researchers who were able to identify users' <a href="/wiki/RFID" class="mw-redirect" title="RFID">RFID</a> devices from 60 feet (18 m) away using small, concealable intelligence <a href="/wiki/Crossbow_Technology" title="Crossbow Technology">motes</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Wireless_sensor_network" title="Wireless sensor network">wireless sensor network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p><p>In 2004, Nike launched the <a href="/wiki/SPARQ_Training" title="SPARQ Training">SPARQ Training Program</a>/Division.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Some of Nike's newest shoes contain <a href="/wiki/Nike_Flywire" title="Nike Flywire">Flywire</a> and Lunarlite Foam to reduce weight.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> The Air Zoom Vomero running shoe, introduced in 2006 and currently in its 11th generation, featured a combination of groundbreaking innovations including a full-length air cushioned sole,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> an external heel counter, a crashpad in the heel for shock absorption, and Fit Frame technology for a stable fit.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup></p><p><b>Nike Vaporfly</b>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nike_Vaporfly_Cut_in_Half.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Nike_Vaporfly_Cut_in_Half.png/220px-Nike_Vaporfly_Cut_in_Half.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1377" data-file-height="795"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 127px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Nike_Vaporfly_Cut_in_Half.png/220px-Nike_Vaporfly_Cut_in_Half.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="127" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nike_Vaporfly_Cut_in_Half.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nike Vaporfly cut in half to show the different layers that make up the base of the shoe. The dark grey line shows the carbon fiber plate.</div></div></div>
<p>The Nike Vaporfly first came out in 2017 and their popularity, along with its performance, prompted a new series of running shoes <sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:02_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-58">[57]</a></sup>. The Vaporfly series has a new technological composition that has revolutionized long distance running since studies have shown that these shoes can improve run times up to 4.2%<sup id="cite_ref-:02_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-58">[57]</a></sup>. The composition of the sole contains a foamy material, Pebax, that Nike has altered and now calls it ZoomX (which can be found in other Nike products as well). Pebax foam can also be found in airplane insulation and is "squishier, bouncier, and lighter" than foams in typical running shoes <sup id="cite_ref-:02_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-58">[57]</a></sup>. In the middle of the ZoomX foam there is a full length carbon fiber plate "designed to generate extra spring in every step" <sup id="cite_ref-:02_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-58">[57]</a></sup>. At the time of this writing Nike had just released its newest product from the Vaporfly line, the Nike ZoomX Vaporfly NEXT%, which was marketed as "the fastest shoe we’ve ever made" using Nike's "two most innovative technologies, Nike ZoomX foam and VaporWeave material" <sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Street_fashions">Street fashions</span></h3>
<table class="box-Primary_sources plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Primary_sources" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>relies too much on <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">references</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research">primary sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please improve this section by adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research">secondary or tertiary sources</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nike_no-show_socks.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Nike_no-show_socks.JPG/220px-Nike_no-show_socks.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2250"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Nike_no-show_socks.JPG/220px-Nike_no-show_socks.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nike_no-show_socks.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nike Elite no-show socks with cushioned sole</div></div></div>
<p>The Nike brand, with its distinct V-shaped logo, quickly became regarded as a status symbol<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> in modern <a href="/wiki/Urban_fashion" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban fashion">urban fashion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hip-hop_fashion" class="mw-redirect" title="Hip-hop fashion">hip-hop fashion</a><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> due to its association with success in sport.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> Beginning in the 1980s, various items of Nike clothing became staples of mainstream American <a href="/wiki/Youth_culture" title="Youth culture">youth fashion</a>, especially tracksuits, <a href="/wiki/Shell_suit" class="mw-redirect" title="Shell suit">shell suits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baseball_cap" title="Baseball cap">baseball caps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_Jordans" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Jordans">Air Jordans</a>, Air Force 1's, and <a href="/wiki/Air_Max" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Max">Air Max</a> running shoes<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> with thick, air cushioned rubber soles and contrasting blue, yellow, green, white, or red trim.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Limited edition sneakers and prototypes with a regional early release were known as <a href="/wiki/Quickstrike_sneakers" class="mw-redirect" title="Quickstrike sneakers">Quickstrikes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> and became highly desirable items<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> for teenage members of the <a href="/wiki/Sneakerhead_subculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Sneakerhead subculture">sneakerhead subculture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></p><p>By the 1990s and <a href="/wiki/2000s_youth_fashion" class="mw-redirect" title="2000s youth fashion">2000s</a>, American and European teenagers<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> associated with the <a href="/wiki/Preppy" title="Preppy">preppy</a><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Popular_clique" class="mw-redirect" title="Popular clique">popular clique</a><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> began combining these sneakers,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leggings" title="Leggings">leggings</a>, sweatpants, <a href="/wiki/Crop_top" title="Crop top">crop tops</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> and tracksuits with regular <a href="/wiki/Casual_chic" class="mw-redirect" title="Casual chic">casual chic</a><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> street clothes<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> such as jeans, skirts, <a href="/wiki/Leg_warmers" class="mw-redirect" title="Leg warmers">leg warmers</a>, slouch socks, and <a href="/wiki/Bomber_jacket" class="mw-redirect" title="Bomber jacket">bomber jackets</a>. Particularly popular<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> were the unisex spandex Nike Tempo <a href="/wiki/Compression_shorts" class="mw-redirect" title="Compression shorts">compression shorts</a><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> worn for cycling and running, which had a mesh lining, waterproofing, and, later in the 2000s, a zip pocket for a <a href="/wiki/Walkman" title="Walkman">Walkman</a> or <a href="/wiki/MP3_player" title="MP3 player">MP3 player</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p><p>From the late 2000s into the <a href="/wiki/2010s_in_fashion" title="2010s in fashion">2010s</a>, Nike Elite <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> socks began to be worn as everyday clothes by hip-hop fans and young children.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> 
Originally plain white or black, these socks had special shock absorbing cushioning in the sole<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> plus a moisture wicking upper weave.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Later, Nike Elite socks became available in bright colors inspired by throwback <a href="/wiki/Basketball_uniform" title="Basketball uniform">basketball uniforms</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> often with contrasting bold abstract designs, images of celebrities,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> and freehand digital print<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> to capitalise upon the emerging nostalgia for <a href="/wiki/1990s_fashion" class="mw-redirect" title="1990s fashion">1990s fashion</a>.
</p><p>In 2015, a new self-lacing shoe was introduced. Called the <a href="/wiki/Nike_Mag" title="Nike Mag">Nike Mag</a>, which are replicas of the shoes featured in <i><a href="/wiki/Back_to_the_Future_Part_II" title="Back to the Future Part II">Back to the Future Part II</a></i>, it had a preliminary limited release, only available by auction with all proceeds going to the <a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Fox_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael J. Fox Foundation">Michael J. Fox Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> This was done again in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></p><p>Nike have introduced a premium line, focused more on streetwear than sports wear called NikeLab.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2017, Nike announced its launch of a plus-size clothing line, which will feature new sizes 1X through 3X on more than 200 products.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> Another significant development at this time was the <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Taylor_All-Stars" title="Chuck Taylor All-Stars">Chuck Taylor All-Star Modern</a>, an update of the classic basketball sneaker that incorporated the circular knit upper and cushioned foam sole of Nike's Air Jordans.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Collectibles">Collectibles</span></h3>
<p>On 23 July 2019 a pair of Nike Inc. running shoes sold for $437,500 at a <a href="/wiki/Sotheby%27s" title="Sotheby's">Sotheby's</a> auction. The so-called "Moon Shoes" were designed by Nike co-founder and track coach Bill Bowerman for runners participating in the 1972 Olympics trials. The buyer was <a href="/wiki/MDC_Partners" title="MDC Partners">Miles Nadal</a>, a Canadian investor and car collector, who had just paid $850,000 for a group of 99 rare of limited collection pairs of sport shoes.  The purchase price was the highest for one pair of sneakers, the previous record being $190,373 in 2017 for a pair of signed <a href="/wiki/Converse_(shoe_company)" title="Converse (shoe company)">Converse</a> shoes in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, said to have been worn by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a> during the 1984 basketball final of the Olympics that year.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Headquarters">Headquarters</span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<p>Nike's world headquarters are surrounded by the city of Beaverton but are within <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated</a> <a href="/wiki/Washington_County,_Oregon" title="Washington County, Oregon">Washington County</a>. The city attempted to forcibly annex Nike's headquarters, which led to a lawsuit by Nike, and <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> by the company that ultimately ended in Oregon Senate Bill 887 of 2005. Under that bill's terms, Beaverton is specifically barred from forcibly annexing the land that Nike and <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Sportswear" title="Columbia Sportswear">Columbia Sportswear</a> occupy in Washington County for 35 years, while <a href="/wiki/Electro_Scientific_Industries" title="Electro Scientific Industries">Electro Scientific Industries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tektronix" title="Tektronix">Tektronix</a> receive the same protection for 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Nike is planning to build a 3.2 million square foot expansion to its World Headquarters in Beaverton.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-90">[89]</a></sup> The design will target LEED Platinum certification and will be highlighted by natural daylight, and a gray water treatment center.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NikeCanada.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/NikeCanada.jpg/220px-NikeCanada.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="4928"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 332px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/NikeCanada.jpg/220px-NikeCanada.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="332" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NikeCanada.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nike office in North America.</div></div></div>
<p>Nike has contracted with more than 700 shops around the world and has offices located in 45 countries outside the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Most of the factories are located in Asia, including Indonesia, China, Taiwan, India,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> Thailand, Vietnam, Pakistan, Philippines, and Malaysia.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Nike is hesitant to disclose information about the contract companies it works with.  However, due to harsh criticism from some organizations like <a href="/wiki/CorpWatch" title="CorpWatch">CorpWatch</a>, Nike has disclosed information about its contract factories in its Corporate Governance Report.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweatshops">Sweatshops</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nike_sweatshops" title="Nike sweatshops">Nike sweatshops</a></div>
<p>Nike has been criticized for contracting with factories (known as <a href="/wiki/Nike_sweatshops" title="Nike sweatshops">Nike sweatshops</a>) in countries such as <a href="/wiki/Labor_relations_in_China" title="Labor relations in China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. Vietnam Labor Watch, an activist group, has documented that factories contracted by Nike have <a href="/wiki/Wage_theft" title="Wage theft">violated</a> <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> and overtime <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Vietnam" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Vietnam">laws in Vietnam</a> as late as 1996, although Nike claims that this practice has been stopped.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>  The company has been subject to much critical coverage of the often poor working conditions and <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Exploitation of labor">exploitation</a> of cheap overseas labor employed in the <a href="/wiki/Free_trade_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Free trade zone">free trade zones</a> where their goods are typically manufactured.  Sources for this criticism include <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Klein" title="Naomi Klein">Naomi Klein</a>'s book <i><a href="/wiki/No_Logo" title="No Logo">No Logo</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a> documentaries.
</p><p>Campaigns have been taken up by many colleges and universities, especially <a href="/wiki/Anti-globalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-globalisation">anti-globalisation</a> groups, as well as several <a href="/wiki/Anti-sweatshop_movement" title="Anti-sweatshop movement">anti-sweatshop groups</a> such as the United Students Against Sweatshops.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></p><p>As of July 2011, Nike stated that two-thirds of its factories producing Converse products still do not meet the company's standards for worker treatment. A July 2011 <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> article stated that employees at the company's plants in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> reported constant abuse from supervisors.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_labor_allegations">Child labor allegations</span></h3>
<p>During the 1990s, Nike faced criticism for the use of <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">child labor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Child_labour_in_Cambodia" title="Child labour in Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_labour_in_Pakistan" title="Child labour in Pakistan">Pakistan</a> in factories it contracted to manufacture soccer balls. Although Nike took action to curb or at least reduce the practice, they continue to contract their production to companies that operate in areas where inadequate regulation and monitoring make it hard to ensure that child labor is not being used.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></p><p>In 2001, a BBC documentary uncovered occurrences of child labor and poor working conditions in a Cambodian factory used by Nike.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> The documentary focused on six girls, who all worked seven days a week, often 16 hours a day.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Strike_in_China_factory">Strike in China factory</span></h3>
<p>In April 2014, one of the biggest strikes in mainland China took place at the <a href="/wiki/Yue_Yuen_Industrial_Holdings" title="Yue Yuen Industrial Holdings">Yue Yuen Industrial Holdings</a> <a href="/wiki/Dongguan" title="Dongguan">Dongguan</a> shoe factory, producing amongst others for Nike. Yue Yuen did underpay an employee by 250 yuan (40.82 US Dollars) per month. The average salary at Yue Yuen is 3000 yuan per month. The factory employs 70,000 people. This practice was in place for nearly 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Paradise_Papers">Paradise Papers</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paradise_Papers" title="Paradise Papers">Paradise Papers</a></div>
<p>On 5 November 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Paradise_Papers" title="Paradise Papers">Paradise Papers</a>, a set of confidential <a href="/wiki/Electronic_document" title="Electronic document">electronic documents</a> relating to <a href="/wiki/Offshore_investment" title="Offshore investment">offshore investment</a>, revealed that Nike is among the corporations that used <a href="/wiki/Offshore_company" title="Offshore company">offshore companies</a> to avoid taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Appleby_(law_firm)" title="Appleby (law firm)">Appleby</a> documents detail how Nike boosted its after-tax profits by, among other maneuvers, transferring ownership of its Swoosh trademark to a Bermudan subsidiary, Nike International Ltd. This transfer allowed the subsidiary to charge royalties to its European headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Hilversum" title="Hilversum">Hilversum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, effectively converting taxable company <a href="/wiki/Profit_(accounting)" title="Profit (accounting)">profits</a> to an account payable in <a href="/wiki/Tax_haven" title="Tax haven">tax-free</a> <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nike_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nike-105">[104]</a></sup> Although the subsidiary was effectively run by executives at Nike's main offices in <a href="/wiki/Beaverton,_Oregon" title="Beaverton, Oregon">Beaverton, Oregon</a>—to the point where a duplicate of the Bermudan company's seal was needed—for tax purposes the subsidiary was treated as Bermuda. Its profits were not declared in Europe and came to light only because of a mostly unrelated case in US Tax Court, where papers filed by Nike briefly mention royalties in 2010, 2011 and 2012 totaling $3.86 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Nike_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nike-105">[104]</a></sup> Under an arrangement with Dutch authorities, the tax break was to expire in 2014, so another reorganization transferred the intellectual property from the Bermudan company to a Dutch <i>commanditaire vennootschap</i> or limited partnership, Nike Innovate CV. Dutch law treats income earned by a CV as if it had been earned by the principals, who owe no tax in the Netherlands if they do not reside there.<sup id="cite_ref-Nike_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nike-105">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Colin_Kaepernick">Colin Kaepernick</span></h3>
<p>In September 2018, Nike announced it had signed former American football quarterback <a href="/wiki/Colin_Kaepernick" title="Colin Kaepernick">Colin Kaepernick</a>, noted for his <a href="/wiki/U.S._national_anthem_protests_(2016%E2%80%93present)" title="U.S. national anthem protests (2016–present)">controversial decision to kneel</a> during the playing of the US national anthem, to a long-term advertising campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> According to Charles Robinson of <a href="/wiki/Yahoo_Sports" title="Yahoo Sports">Yahoo! Sports</a>, Kaepernick and Nike agreed to a new contract despite the fact Kaepernick has been with the company since 2011 and said that "interest from other shoe companies" played a part in the new agreement. Robinson said the contract is a "wide endorsement" where Kaepernick will have his own branded line including shoes, shirts, jerseys and more. According to Robinson, Kaepernick signed a "star" contract that puts him level with a "top-end NFL player" worth millions per year plus royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> In response, some people set fire to their own Nike-branded clothes and shoes or cut the Nike swoosh logo out of their clothes, and the <a href="/wiki/Fraternal_Order_of_Police" title="Fraternal Order of Police">Fraternal Order of Police</a> called the advertisement an "insult";<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vibe_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-110">[109]</a></sup> others, such as <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Serena_Williams" title="Serena Williams">Serena Williams</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Black_Police_Association_(United_States)" title="National Black Police Association (United States)">National Black Police Association</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-vibe_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibe-110">[109]</a></sup> praised Nike for its campaign. The <a href="/wiki/College_of_the_Ozarks" title="College of the Ozarks">College of the Ozarks</a> removed Nike from all their athletic uniforms in response.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup></p><p>During the following week, Nike's stock price fell 2.2%, even as online orders of Nike products rose 27% compared with the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> In the following three months, Nike reported a rise in sales.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2019, Nike released a shoe featuring a <a href="/wiki/Betsy_Ross_flag" title="Betsy Ross flag">Betsy Ross flag</a> called the Air Max 1 Quick Strike Fourth of July trainers. The trainers were designed to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a>. The model was subsequently withdrawn after <a href="/wiki/Colin_Kaepernick" title="Colin Kaepernick">Colin Kaepernick</a> told the brand he and others found the flag offensive because of its association with slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-safdar2019_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safdar2019-116">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-complaint_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-complaint-118">[117]</a></sup></p><p>Nike's decision to withdraw the product drew criticism from <a href="/wiki/List_of_governors_of_Arizona" title="List of governors of Arizona">Arizona's</a> Republican Governor, <a href="/wiki/Doug_Ducey" title="Doug Ducey">Doug Ducey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Senators_from_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United States Senators from Texas">Texas</a>'s Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> Nike's decision was praised by others due to the use of the flag by <a href="/wiki/White_nationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="White nationalists">white nationalists</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-complaint_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-complaint-118">[117]</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Defamation_League" title="Anti-Defamation League">Anti-Defamation League's</a> Center on Extremism has declined to add the flag to its database of "hate symbols."<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong_protests">Hong Kong protests</span></h3>
<p>U.S. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a> criticized Nike for "siding with the Chinese Communist Party and silencing free speech".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Nike_Vaporfly_Shoe">Nike Vaporfly Shoe</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nike_Vaporfly_and_Tokyo_2020_Olympics_Controversy" title="Nike Vaporfly and Tokyo 2020 Olympics Controversy">Nike Vaporfly and Tokyo 2020 Olympics Controversy</a></div>
<p>On January 31, 2020, the <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics" title="World Athletics">World Athletics</a> issued new guidelines concerning shoes to be used in the upcoming <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">Tokyo 2020 Olympics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-122">[121]</a></sup> These updates came in response to criticisms concerning technology in the Nike Vaporfly running shoes, which had been submitted beginning around 2017-2018.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-123">[122]</a></sup> These criticisms stated that the shoes provided athletes with an unfair advantage over their opponents and some critics considered it to be a form of <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_doping" title="Mechanical doping">mechanical doping</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:03_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-124">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-125">[124]</a></sup> According to Nike funded research, the shoes can improve efficiency by up to 4.2%<sup id="cite_ref-:03_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:03-124">[123]</a></sup> and runners who have tested the shoe are saying that it causes reduced soreness in the legs; sports technologist Bryce Dyer attributes this to the ZoomX and carbon fiber plate since it absorbs the energy and "spring[s] runners forward".<sup id="cite_ref-:3_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-125">[124]</a></sup> Some athletes, scientists, and fans have compared this to the 2008 <a href="/wiki/LZR_Racer" title="LZR Racer">LAZR swimsuit controversy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup></p><p>Some of the major changes in the guidelines that have come about as a result of these criticisms include that the "sole must be no thicker than 40mm" and that "the shoe must not contain more than one rigid embedded plate or blade (of any material) that runs either the full length or only part of the length of the shoe. The plate may be in more than one part but those parts must be located sequentially in one plane (not stacked or in parallel) and must not overlap". The components of the shoes are not the only thing that had major changes; starting April 30th, 2020, "any shoe must have been available for purchase by any athlete on the open retail market (online or in store) for a period of four months before it can be used in competition".<sup id="cite_ref-:12_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-122">[121]</a></sup> Prior to these new guidelines World Athletics reviewed the Vaporfly shoes and "concluded that there is independent research that indicates that the new technology incorporated in the soles of road and spiked shoes may provide a performance advantage" and that it recommends further research to "establish the true impact of [the Vaporfly] technology."<sup id="cite_ref-:12_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_record">Environmental record</span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>According to the New England-based environmental organization Clean Air-Cool Planet, Nike ranked among the top three companies (out of 56) in a survey of climate-friendly companies in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> Nike has also been praised for its <a href="/wiki/Nike_Grind" title="Nike Grind">Nike Grind</a> program (which closes the <a href="/wiki/Product_lifecycle_(marketing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Product lifecycle (marketing)">product lifecycle</a>) by groups like <a href="/wiki/Climate_Counts" title="Climate Counts">Climate Counts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> One campaign that Nike began for Earth Day 2008 was a commercial that featured basketball star <a href="/wiki/Steve_Nash" title="Steve Nash">Steve Nash</a> wearing Nike's Trash Talk Shoe, which had been constructed in February 2008 from pieces of leather and synthetic leather waste from factory floors. The Trash Talk Shoe also featured a sole composed of ground-up rubber from a shoe recycling program. Nike claims this is the first performance basketball shoe that has been created from manufacturing waste, but it only produced 5,000 pairs for sale.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup></p><p>Another project Nike has begun is called Nike's Reuse-A-Shoe program. This program, started in 1993, is Nike's longest-running program that benefits both the environment and the community by collecting old athletic shoes of any type in order to process and recycle them. The material that is produced is then used to help create sports surfaces such as basketball courts, running tracks, and playgrounds.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup></p><p>A project through the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill" title="University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill">University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill</a> found workers were exposed to toxic <a href="/wiki/Isocyanate" title="Isocyanate">isocyanates</a> and other chemicals in footwear factories in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. In addition to inhalation, dermal exposure was the biggest problem found.  This could result in allergic reactions including asthmatic reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing_strategy">Marketing strategy</span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<p>Nike promotes its products by <a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">sponsorship agreements</a> with celebrity athletes, professional teams and college athletic teams.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span></h3>
<p>In 1982, Nike aired its first three national television ads, created by newly formed ad agency <a href="/wiki/Wieden%2BKennedy" title="Wieden+Kennedy">Wieden+Kennedy</a> (W+K), during the broadcast of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Marathon" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Marathon">New York Marathon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> The Cannes Advertising Festival has named Nike its Advertiser of the Year in 1994 and 2003, making it the first company to receive that honor twice.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></p><p>Nike also has earned the <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a> for best commercial twice since the award was first created in the 1990s. The first was for "The Morning After," a satirical look at what a runner might face on the morning of January 1, 2000 if every dire prediction about the <a href="/wiki/Year_2000_problem" title="Year 2000 problem">Y2K problem</a> came to fruition.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> The second was for a 2002 spot called "Move," which featured a series of famous and everyday athletes in a variety of athletic pursuits.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Beatles_song">Beatles song</span></h4>
<p>Nike was criticized for its use of the <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">Beatles</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Revolution_(Beatles_song)" title="Revolution (Beatles song)">Revolution</a>" in a 1987 commercial against the wishes of <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">Apple Records</a>, the Beatles' recording company. Nike paid US$250,000 to <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Records" title="Capitol Records">Capitol Records</a> Inc., which held the North American licensing rights to the recordings, for the right to use the Beatles' rendition for a year.
</p><p>Apple Records sued Nike Inc., Capitol Records Inc., <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI Records</a> Inc. and Wieden+Kennedy for $15 million.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup>  Capitol-EMI countered by saying the lawsuit was "groundless" because Capitol had licensed the use of "Revolution" with the "active support and encouragement of <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">Yoko Ono</a>, a shareholder and director of Apple Records."
</p><p>Nike discontinued airing ads featuring "Revolution" in March 1988. Yoko Ono later gave permission to Nike to use <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>'s "Instant Karma" in another advertisement.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="New_media_marketing">New media marketing</span></h4>
<p>Nike was an early adopter of <a href="/wiki/Internet_marketing" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet marketing">internet marketing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Email_management" title="Email management">email management</a> technologies, and using <a href="/wiki/Broadcast" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadcast">broadcast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narrowcasting" title="Narrowcasting">narrowcast</a> communication technologies to create multimedia marketing campaigns.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_Threat_advertisement">Minor Threat advertisement</span></h4><p>
In late June 2005, Nike received criticism from <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacKaye" title="Ian MacKaye">Ian MacKaye</a>, owner of <a href="/wiki/Dischord_Records" title="Dischord Records">Dischord Records</a>, guitarist/vocalist for <a href="/wiki/Fugazi" title="Fugazi">Fugazi</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Evens" title="The Evens">The Evens</a>, and front man of the defunct punk band <a href="/wiki/Minor_Threat" title="Minor Threat">Minor Threat</a>, for appropriating imagery and text from Minor Threat's <a href="/wiki/First_Two_7%22s_on_a_12%22" class="mw-redirect" title='First Two 7"s on a 12"'>1981 self-titled album</a>'s cover art in a flyer promoting <a href="/wiki/Nike_Skateboarding" title="Nike Skateboarding">Nike Skateboarding</a>'s 2005 East Coast demo tour. </p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>On June 27, Nike Skateboarding's website issued an apology to Dischord, Minor Threat, and fans of both and announced that they have tried to remove and dispose of all flyers. They stated that the people who designed it were skateboarders and Minor Threat fans themselves who created the advertisement out of respect and appreciation for the band.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup>  The dispute was eventually settled out of court between Nike and Minor Threat.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Niketown08.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Niketown08.jpg/220px-Niketown08.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1224"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Niketown08.jpg/220px-Niketown08.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Niketown08.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Niketown at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Circus" title="Oxford Circus">Oxford Circus</a>, London</div></div></div>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Nike_6.0">Nike 6.0</span></h4>
<p>As part of the 6.0 campaign, Nike introduced a new line of T-shirts that include phrases such as "Dope", "Get High" and "Ride Pipe" – sports lingo that is also a <a href="/wiki/Double_entendre" title="Double entendre">double entendre</a> for <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">drug use</a>. Boston Mayor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Menino" title="Thomas Menino">Thomas Menino</a> expressed his objection to the shirts after seeing them in a window display at the city's Niketown and asked the store to remove the display. "What we don't need is a major corporation like Nike, which tries to appeal to the younger generation, out there giving credence to the drug issue," Menino told <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Herald" class="mw-redirect" title="The Boston Herald">The Boston Herald</a></i>. A company official stated the shirts were meant to pay homage to extreme sports, and that Nike does not condone the illegal use of drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> Nike was forced to replace the shirt line.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="NBA_uniform_deal">NBA uniform deal</span></h3>
<p>In June 2015, Nike signed an 8-year deal with the <a href="/wiki/NBA" class="mw-redirect" title="NBA">NBA</a> to become the official uniform supplier for the league, beginning with the 2017–18 season. The brand took over for <a href="/wiki/Adidas" title="Adidas">Adidas</a>, who provided the uniforms for the league since 2006. Unlike previous deals, Nike's logo appear on NBA jerseys – a first for the league.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> The only exception is the <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Hornets" title="Charlotte Hornets">Charlotte Hornets</a>, owned by longtime Nike endorser <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a>, which instead uses the <a href="/wiki/Jumpman_(logo)" title="Jumpman (logo)">Jumpman</a> logo associated with Jordan-related merchandise.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Sponsorship">Sponsorship</span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nike_sponsorships" title="List of Nike sponsorships">List of Nike sponsorships</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg/170px-Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="385"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 227px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg/170px-Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="227" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a> (pictured in 1987) helped drive Nike sales</div></div></div>
<p>Nike sponsors top athletes in many sports to use their products and <a href="/wiki/Promotion_(marketing)" title="Promotion (marketing)">promote</a> and advertise their technology and design. Nike's first professional athlete endorser was Romanian tennis player <a href="/wiki/Ilie_N%C4%83stase" title="Ilie Năstase">Ilie Năstase</a>. The first track endorser was distance runner <a href="/wiki/Steve_Prefontaine" title="Steve Prefontaine">Steve Prefontaine</a>. Prefontaine was the prized pupil of the company's co-founder, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Bowerman" title="Bill Bowerman">Bill Bowerman</a>, while he coached at the University of Oregon. Today, the Steve Prefontaine Building is named in his honor at Nike's corporate headquarters. Nike has only made one statue of its sponsored athletes and it is of Steve Prefontaine.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup></p><p>Nike has also sponsored many other successful <a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track and field</a> athletes over the years, such as <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Coe" title="Sebastian Coe">Sebastian Coe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Lewis" title="Carl Lewis">Carl Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Joyner-Kersee" title="Jackie Joyner-Kersee">Jackie Joyner-Kersee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Johnson_(sprinter)" title="Michael Johnson (sprinter)">Michael Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allyson_Felix" title="Allyson Felix">Allyson Felix</a>.  The signing of basketball player <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan" title="Michael Jordan">Michael Jordan</a> in 1984, with his subsequent promotion of Nike over the course of his career, with <a href="/wiki/Spike_Lee" title="Spike Lee">Spike Lee</a> as <a href="/wiki/Mars_Blackmon" title="Mars Blackmon">Mars Blackmon</a>, proved to be one of the biggest boosts to Nike's publicity and sales.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ronaldinho_11feb2007.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Ronaldinho_11feb2007.jpg/170px-Ronaldinho_11feb2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="563" data-file-height="675"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 204px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Ronaldinho_11feb2007.jpg/170px-Ronaldinho_11feb2007.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="204" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ronaldinho_11feb2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ronaldinho" title="Ronaldinho">Ronaldinho</a> (pictured with Barcelona in 2007) appeared in a 2005 Nike advertisement that went <a href="/wiki/Viral_video" title="Viral video">viral</a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>, becoming the site's first video to reach one million views.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Nike is a major sponsor of the athletic programs at <a href="/wiki/Penn_State_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Penn State University">Penn State University</a> and named its first child care facility after <a href="/wiki/Joe_Paterno" title="Joe Paterno">Joe Paterno</a> when it opened in 1990 at the company's headquarters. Nike originally announced it would not remove Paterno's name from the building in the wake of the Penn State sex abuse scandal. After the Freeh Report was released on July 12, 2012, Nike CEO Mark Parker announced the name Joe Paterno would be removed immediately from the child development center. A new name has yet to be announced.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kylian_Mbappe_2017.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kylian_Mbappe_2017.jpg/170px-Kylian_Mbappe_2017.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="276" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1188" data-file-height="1929"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 276px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kylian_Mbappe_2017.jpg/170px-Kylian_Mbappe_2017.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="276" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kylian_Mbappe_2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nike_Hypervenom" title="Nike Hypervenom">Nike Hypervenom</a> 3 boots were commissioned for French prodigy <a href="/wiki/Kylian_Mbapp%C3%A9" title="Kylian Mbappé">Kylian Mbappé</a></div></div></div>
<p>in the early 1990s Nike made a strong push into the association football business making endorsement deals with famous and charismatic players such as <a href="/wiki/Romario" class="mw-redirect" title="Romario">Romario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Cantona" title="Eric Cantona">Eric Cantona</a> or <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Davids" title="Edgar Davids">Edgar Davids</a>. They continued the growth in the sport by signing more top players including: <a href="/wiki/Ronaldo_(Brazilian_footballer)" title="Ronaldo (Brazilian footballer)">Ronaldo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronaldinho" title="Ronaldinho">Ronaldinho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Totti" title="Francesco Totti">Francesco Totti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Henry" title="Thierry Henry">Thierry Henry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Didier_Drogba" title="Didier Drogba">Didier Drogba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Iniesta" title="Andrés Iniesta">Andrés Iniesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Rooney" title="Wayne Rooney">Wayne Rooney</a> and still have many of the sport's biggest stars under their name, with  <a href="/wiki/Cristiano_Ronaldo" title="Cristiano Ronaldo">Cristiano Ronaldo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zlatan_Ibrahimovi%C4%87" title="Zlatan Ibrahimović">Zlatan Ibrahimović</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neymar" title="Neymar">Neymar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Kane" title="Harry Kane">Harry Kane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eden_Hazard" title="Eden Hazard">Eden Hazard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kylian_Mbapp%C3%A9" title="Kylian Mbappé">Kylian Mbappé</a> among others.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup></p><p>In 2012, Nike carried a commercial partnership with the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Football_Confederation" title="Asian Football Confederation">Asian Football Confederation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> 
In August 2014, Nike announced that they will not renew their kit supply deal with Manchester United after the 2014–15 season, citing rising costs.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> Since the start of the 2015–16 season, Adidas has manufactured Manchester United's kit as part of a world-record 10-year deal worth a minimum of £750 million.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> Nike still has many of the top teams playing in their uniforms, including: <a href="/wiki/FC_Barcelona" title="FC Barcelona">FC Barcelona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris_Saint-Germain_F.C." title="Paris Saint-Germain F.C.">Paris Saint-Germain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a> (the latter from the 2020–21 season),<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup> and the national teams of Brazil, France, England, Portugal and the Netherlands among many others. 
</p><p>Nike has been the sponsor for many top ranked tennis players. Brand's commercial success in the sport went hand in hand with the endorsement deals signed with the biggest and the world's most charismatic stars and number one ranked players of the subsequent eras, including <a href="/wiki/John_McEnroe" title="John McEnroe">John McEnroe</a> in the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Andre_Agassi" title="Andre Agassi">Andre Agassi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pete_Sampras" title="Pete Sampras">Pete Sampras</a> in the 1990s and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Federer" title="Roger Federer">Roger Federer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Nadal" title="Rafael Nadal">Rafael Nadal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serena_Williams" title="Serena Williams">Serena Williams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sharapova" title="Maria Sharapova">Maria Sharapova</a> with the start of the XXI century.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup></p><p>Nike has sponsored <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Woods" title="Tiger Woods">Tiger Woods</a> for much of his career, and remained on his side amid the controversies that shaped the golfer's career.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> In January 2013, Nike signed <a href="/wiki/Rory_McIlroy" title="Rory McIlroy">Rory McIlroy</a>, the then No 1 golfer in the world to a 10-year sponsorship deal worth $250 million. The deal includes using Nike's range of <a href="/wiki/Golf_club" title="Golf club">golf clubs</a>, a move <a href="/wiki/Nick_Faldo" title="Nick Faldo">Nick Faldo</a> previously described as "dangerous" for McIlroy's game.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-156">[155]</a></sup></p><p>Nike has been the official kit sponsor for the <a href="/wiki/Indian_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian cricket team">Indian cricket team</a> since 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> On February 21, 2013, Nike announced it suspended its contract with South African limbless athlete <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Pistorius" title="Oscar Pistorius">Oscar Pistorius</a>, due to his being charged with <a href="/wiki/Premeditated_murder" class="mw-redirect" title="Premeditated murder">premeditated murder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup></p><p>Nike consolidated its position in basketball in 2015 when it was announced that the company would sign an 8-year deal with the NBA, taking over from the league's previous uniform sponsor, Adidas. The deal required all franchise team members to wear jerseys and shorts with the Swoosh logo, beginning with the 2017/18 season.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> After the success of partnership with Jordan, which resulted in the creation of the unique <a href="/wiki/Air_Jordan" title="Air Jordan">Air Jordan</a> brand, Nike has continued to build partnership with the biggest names in basketball. Some have had signature shoes designed for them, including <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Bryant" title="Kobe Bryant">Kobe Bryant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jason_Kidd" title="Jason Kidd">Jason Kidd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vince_Carter" title="Vince Carter">Vince Carter</a> and more recently <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Durant" title="Kevin Durant">Kevin Durant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_George" title="Paul George">Paul George</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup></p><p>A news report originating from CNN reported that Nike spent $11.5 billion, nearly a third of its sales, on marketing and endorsement contracts in the year 2018. Nike and its Jordan brand sponsored 85 men's and women's basketball teams in the NCAA tournament.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Ties_with_the_University_of_Oregon">Ties with the University of Oregon</span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Ducks#Relationship_with_Nike" title="Oregon Ducks">Oregon Ducks § Relationship with Nike</a></div>
<p>The company maintains strong ties, both directly and indirectly (through partnership with <a href="/wiki/Phil_Knight" title="Phil Knight">Phil Knight</a>), with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oregon" title="University of Oregon">University of Oregon</a>. Nike designs the University of Oregon football program's team attire. New unique combinations are issued before every game day.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tinker_Hatfield" title="Tinker Hatfield">Tinker Hatfield</a>, who also redesigned the university's logo, leads this effort.<sup id="cite_ref-Knight,_and_Tinker_Hatfield_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knight,_and_Tinker_Hatfield-168">[167]</a></sup></p><p>More recently, the corporation donated $13.5 million towards the renovation and expansion of <a href="/wiki/Hayward_Field" title="Hayward Field">Hayward Field</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup></p><p>Phil Knight has invested substantial personal funds towards developing and maintaining the university's athletic apparatus.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> His university projects often involve input from Nike designers and executives, such as <a href="/wiki/Tinker_Hatfield" title="Tinker Hatfield">Tinker Hatfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Knight,_and_Tinker_Hatfield_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knight,_and_Tinker_Hatfield-168">[167]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">
<p>In 2012, Nike is listed as a partner of the <a href="/wiki/Product_Red" title="Product Red">(PRODUCT)<sup>RED</sup></a> campaign together with other brands such as <a href="/wiki/Girl_Distribution_Company" title="Girl Distribution Company">Girl</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Express" title="American Express">American Express</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Converse_(shoe_company)" title="Converse (shoe company)">Converse</a>. The campaign's mission is to prevent the transmission of the HIV virus from mother to child by 2015 (the campaign's byline is "Fighting For An AIDS Free Generation").  The company's goal is to raise and send funds, for education and medical assistance to those who live in areas heavily effected by AIDS.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(10)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Program">Program</span></h2><section class="mf-section-10 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-10">
<p>The Nike Community Ambassador Program, allows Nike employees from around the world to go out and give to their community.  Over 3,900 employees from various Nike stores have participated in teaching children to be active and healthy.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(11)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2><section class="mf-section-11 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-11">

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(12)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2><section class="mf-section-12 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-12">

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references"><li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The pronunciations of "Nike"<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> include <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">NY</span>-kee</i></a> officially and in the US, as well as  <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">NYKE</span></i></a> outside of the US.</span>
</li>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(13)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2><section class="mf-section-13 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-13">
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
